B007P6VLR8|2.0|...the fact that the vinyl version does not come with a free download card or link. That averages to two stars overall.<br /><br />Firstly the music. Overall, it's pretty good but nothing special. All this talk about \\"return to form\\" or \\"best album since Mellon Collie\\" is balderdash, but I am also willing to admit that I will probably need 3-5 attentive listens for things to properly sink in (the best since Mellon Collie is the criminally underrated Machina 1). This record falls in line with the 2 and a half previously released Teargarden EPs-decent, but not outstanding and nowhere near as good as the prior catalog. Nicole Fiorentino does stand out with some excellent bass playing (if it is in fact her; the long standing rumor is that Billy plays all of the instruments except drums on the recordings)-I was taken aback by how prominent in the mix and skillfully played the bass is on this record. But where this record really suffers-as do the other Teargarden EPs-is in the lack of Jimmy Chamberlin. I'm sorry and no disrespect to Mike Byrne (who does a commendable job here), but Jimmy's distinctly frenetic, loose, and jazzy playing is a key component to the Pumpkins sound. I expect this record to get better and more rewarding with repeated listens, but my first impressions hint that this will not be classic Pumpkins record in the long run.<br /><br />Now onto this whole vinyl/mp3 issue. Every major new vinyl purchase I've made this year (Garbage's Not Your Kind Of People, Sigur Ros' Valtari, Anathema's Weather Systems, and Storm Corrosion's self-titled album as a few of many examples), as well as most in the recent past years, comes with some sort of card or code by where you can download the corresponding record for your mp3 player free of charge. When I go digging through bins at stores, I notice big stickers plastered on the vast majority of newly released vinyl of bands I don't even care about that indicate this feature as well. It's a reasonable assertion that theinclusion of a free download with a vinyl purchase was becoming something of an industry standard. So why not with this record? I'm really annoyed and disappointed with this, and would give zero stars to this aspect if I could (averaging one and a half stars for the record overall, rounded down to one star to conform to Amazon's rating system). I very likely would have never purchased the $27 (after shipping) vinyl copy (especially now knowing how mediocre the music is) and simply purchased the $5 (as of this writing) download instead-the $22 is now a sunk cost to me. Now I'm forced to do one of three things: 1) Attempt to rip the vinyl, but that's far too much work for a substandard product, 2) Acquire the mp3s illicitly, which I don't plan to do as I don't steal online, or 3) very begrudgingly pay the additional money for the mp3 copy, which I see as a veiled form of price gouging. The vinyl presentation itself is beautiful (a thick cardboard gatefold and 180g heavyweight vinyl) and sounds fantastic, but man I am not happy about essentially being forced to buy the album twice in order to enjoy it anywhere besides my home stereo.
B0079XPUOW|3.0|Here's the deal with this book, spoiler-free: it's a Star Trek parody about how the unimportant characters always get killed off. The prologue is hysterical. After that, it's pretty funny as long as it's doing its Star Trek parody thing. Where it starts to lose steam is when it gets meta (I'm not going to go into this, because I don't want to ruin anything for anyone). Given what the author was doing, there was no way to avoid this, and it's still a fairly entertaining (and often funny) novel even after it eases off the parody and begins to tell its own story. My only real critique is that the book should have ended when the story ended. It didn't. Instead, there are three \\"Codas\\" following the end of the main story. They lose all of their sense of humor and try to get serious about a novel that had been entirely tongue-in-cheek. They don't work and all and a novel that could have ended on a light, fun note (as the main story did) instead ends with three consecutive bellyflops.<br /><br />Still, it's not enough to ruin the book, which was overall pretty darn enjoyable. It's a very, very quick read. You'll finish it in an afternoon or two. In the end, if you enjoy Star Trek or just want to read some less-serious science fiction, Redshirts is a good bet. It's not great but it's good enough that I will be reading some of Scalzi's other books.

0765316994|3.0|There are books that you read, and while the book itself was fun and enjoyable, you get to the end and get a little bit of an emotional sucker punch. And sometimes that's just what you want. Other times, you find yourself wishing that the emotional core might have occupied more of the landscape.<br /><br />Scalzi's Redshirts is one of those books. It's fluffy, it's funny, it wallows in the meta, and at the tale's end, there's a little bit of emotion pinging your heart. I'm not sure if it worked fully for me. I really enjoyed the three codas, and the strongest characterisation of the book occurs in them, but like an earlier Scalzi novel, Agent to the Stars, the emotional right turn feels at odds with the rest of the book's breezy, assuredly light but confident tone.<br /><br />Truth be told, I prefer Scalzi when his humor is sublimated a little bit further into the story. I prefer Old Man's War, Ghost Brigades, Last Colony, and Zoe's Tale to his more light-hearted fare like Agent and Android's Dream. But I did heartily enjoy his reading of the first chapter while on tour in Seattle, and when it came out, it was an auto-buy. His send-up of the Star Trek tropes was amusing and if you're looking for nothing more than that, you'll probably be quite satisfied. I was still pretty entertained over-all, even if this isn't climbing into my favourites' shelf anytime soon.

0765316994|3.0|Ah, redshirts. Any geek worth their salt knows about them -- random extras on \\"Star Trek\\" who die in almost every episode.<br /><br />But what if the redshirts knew that their fates were coming, and tried to stop it by whatever means necessary? That's the idea behind \\"Redshirts: A Novel with Three Codas,\\" John Scalzi's affectionate lampooning of sci-fi TV. It's a light, fluffy novel that touches on some philosophical ideas, but nothing too serious.<br /><br />In the 23rd century, Ensign Andrew Dahl is assigned to the Universal Union flagship Intrepid. It's a dream job for a xenobiologist, except that some of his new shipmates are acting a little strange.<br /><br />And soon he finds out why: the Intrepid has an incredibly high mortality rate. The captain, science officer, engineer, doctor and the handsome if dim Lieutenant Kerensky are never permanently injured, but low-ranking officers inevitably die messy, violent deaths. There's also a magic \\"box\\" that generates the answer to impossible problems.<br /><br />The widower of one \\"redshirt\\" has already figured out the answer -- a bizarre answer that calls into question the nature of reality itself. And with Dahl scheduled for an away mission, he and his fellow endangered officers will have to take drastic steps: go back in time to a parallel world, and find a way of stopping their problems in the 21st century.<br /><br />\\"Redshirts\\" is a fun idea for a story -- what if the disposable extras became aware of their doomedness, and tried to prevent it? There's a lot of metafictional twists and some contemplation of what reality is, but it never gets too heavy. It's a pleasantly light sci-fi story.<br /><br />It also has some fun with the scientific improbabilities of \\"Star Trek\\" and similar shows, although the world of the Intrepid is definitely much, much dumber than most (robots with harpoons!). And Scalzi inserts some rather un-\\"Star Trek\\"-like comedy at times, such as Kerensky (and his doppelganger) being deprived of their pants.<br /><br />However, it can get a bit confusing at times. And even now, I'm not entirely sure how you can time-travel into a reality controlling your own without that reality interfering in your plans. Scalzi... sort of explains it at the end, but also doesn't.<br /><br />None of the characters are very deeply developed or given much backstory, but I guess that makes sense since they are meant to be \\"extras\\" who have little development. Scalzi still manages to give them depth and texture that makes them likable, even doofs like Kerensky or the acid-tongued Duvall.<br /><br />\\"Redshirts\\" is a lightweight sci-fi adventure, but the gentle spoofery and humor make it a fun read. Just don't expect great things.their pants.<br /><br />However, it can get a bit confusing at times. Just don't expect great things.
